                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20materials">cathode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v22o2f&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V2%28PO4%292O2F">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Binder&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_binder">binder</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrode+Kinetics&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrode%20kinetics">electrode kinetics</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Up to now, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been playing a key role and holding an overwhelming majority of market of the secondary battery field, since the issue of fossil energy supply and energy conversion became more and more severe. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> A great amount of lithium resources are intensely needed because of the extensive application of LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> However, the storage of lithium resources in the world is quite limited (∼0.0065%), further increasing the cost of LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Thus, more and more attention has been paid to sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), expecting that they can be a competitive substitute to LIBs in large-scale energy storage systems. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Furthermore, the element content of sodium in the earth’s crust is 2.64%, which is nearly 440 times more abundant than that of lithium. Moreover, there is no reaction between sodium and aluminum foil, meaning that the cheaper aluminum can be used to replace the expensive copper current collector in SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> With all of the advantages mentioned above, a number of sodium-based cathode materials are being studied for SIBs, such as NaMO<sub>2</sub> (M = V, Ni, Mn, Cr, Al, and Co), Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 13 to 17">(13−17)</a> NaFePO<sub>4</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>(Fe,Mn)PO<sub>4</sub>F, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>F<sub>3–2<i>x</i></sub> (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 26 to 29">(26−29)</a> Among these cathode materials, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF) has been paid great attention owing to its high voltage plateaus (at 3.6 and 4.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and relatively high theoretical capacity (130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with two Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction during charge/discharge processes). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Moreover, NVPOF with a NASICON structure can provide a stable crystal structural framework and fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths, endowing it with great potential to be an excellent cathode material with favorable cycling stability and rate capability. However, there are still several critical problems that should be solved before its application, including the capacity decay, inferior rate capability, and polarization increase, which originate from the intrinsically poor electronic conductivity of NVPOF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> To obtain better electrochemical performances, many strategies are employed, such as morphological engineering, metal ion doping, and surface modification. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32−34)</a> Although these methods can improve the performance of materials, the composition of electrodes can also affect their electrochemical properties, which is often neglected. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> For instance, a binder is one of the important factors to keep the stability of the electrode structure, but research on binders is relatively scarce. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> A binder can closely combine the active material, conductive additive, and current collector, which enhances the interface contact of the active material and conductive additive/current collector and stabilizes the structure of the electrode, thus influencing the electrochemical properties of the electrode. Over the past decades, as the most commonly used binder, poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) has shown many advantages, such as good electrochemical stability, binding capability, and high adhesion to active materials and current collectors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> However, there are also some shortcomings of using PVDF; in general, the matching solvent will always be <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP), which is highly toxic and can cause severe environmental pollution and great harm to people’s health. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> In addition, both PVDF and NMP are very expensive, which increases the cost of the batteries. Furthermore, PVDF tends to swell and become soft in ester-based electrolytes, meaning that it cannot provide enough cohesiveness between the active material and the current collector, especially in the later period of the long-term cycling process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Under these circumstances, much attention is focused on aqueous binders, such as polyacrylic latex (LA133), sodium alginate (SA), and sodium carboxymethylcellulose (CMC), since they are inexpensive, environmentally friendly, non-flammable, and highly conductive. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> At present, aqueous binders are widely applied in various electrode materials, such as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>F<sub>3–2<i>x</i></sub> (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref27" aria-label="reference 17 and 27">(17,27)</a> and in most of the anode materials, such as Si, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> SiO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> TiO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> and SnS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> which indicates their superior electrochemical properties. However, the influence of different binders on the electrochemical performance of electrode materials gets less attention.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, taking the NVPOF cathode as an example, the effects of four different binders (PVDF, CMC, LA133, and SA) on its electrochemical performance are compared in detail. The results show that the binder can directly impact the rate capacity and cycling stability of the NVPOF cathode. Obviously, using SA as the binder, the NVPOF electrode exhibits the most outstanding rate capacity and cycling stability, such as a super capacity retention of 82 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 20C and excellent cycling stability after 600 cycles at a 5C rate with a capacity retention of 89.2%, which is due to the uniform interspersion of the active material (NVPOF) and carbon black among electrodes, good integrity, low polarization, and superior kinetic properties of the NVPOF electrode. In addition, scanning electron microscopy (SEM), galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD), cyclic voltammetry (CV), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were also employed to illuminate the reasons for the effects of different binders. The results indicate that when SA is used as the binder, NVPOF distributes uniformly in the electrode and the electrode shows good integrity after cycling, due to the abundant carboxyl and hydroxyl groups in the SA binder that have high adhesive and cohesive properties, which reduces the polarization and improves the kinetic properties of the NVPOF electrode, thus obtaining an excellent performance. Moreover, when coupled with a hard carbon anode, the fabricated sodium-ion full batteries (SIFBs) also exhibit an excellent rate capacity, thus providing a preview of their practical application. The strategy of exploring a suitable binder can be widely applied to other cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The NVPOF cathode materials were prepared using a hydrothermal method according to our report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> To prepare the carbon cloth (CC) anode, a commercially available CC was calcined at 1200 °C for 2 h under an Ar atmosphere. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure was analyzed by a powder X-ray diffraction diffractometer (XRD, Rigaku SmartLab) with Cu-Kα radiation (λ = 1.5418 Å) at 40 kV. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Hitachi SU 8000) was performed to observe the morphology of the as-prepared materials. The XPS results were obtained on a VG MultiLab 2000 system with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source (Thermo VG Scientific). Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were recorded on a JEOL-2100F at 100 kV. The Fourier transform infrared spectrum (FTIR Nicolet 6700 spectrometer (Thermo)) was used to analyze the chemical bonds of the as-obtained NVPOF material.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The working electrodes were made by mixing an electrode slurry containing active materials, carbon black, and binders (SA, CMC, PVDF, and LA133) with a weight ratio of 7:2:1 on Al foil, respectively, and then vacuum drying at 60 °C for 12 h. The electrolyte was 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a solution of propylene carbonate with 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate. A glass fiber filter was used as the separator, and metallic sodium was used as both the counter and reference electrodes in the half-cells. GCD measurements were conducted on battery testing systems in the potential window of 2.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at different rates. CV was performed on an electrochemical analyzer at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and GITT was used to study the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Full cells were assembled by coupling the NVPOF cathode with the CC anode. The specific capacities of the full cell were calculated according to the mass of cathode materials based on the voltage window of 2.0–4.3 V. Prior to assembling the full cells, the CC anodes were presodiated to alleviate the initial irreversible capacity loss of full cells. A small amount of the electrolyte was dropped onto the surface of the sodium metal; then, the CC anode was placed on the sodium metal (ensuring that the sodium metal is in complete contact with the CC anode). They were kept in this state for 1.5 h to complete the presodiation of the CC anode.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The NVPOF material was prepared by a simple hydrothermal method, as recently reported by us. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> First, the structural and morphological characteristics of NVPOF were studied by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD), Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy, scanning electron microscopy (SEM), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The phase purity and crystal structure of NASICON-structured NVPOF was first examined through the XRD test. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, all of the diffraction peaks are well consistent with the standard JCPDS card of NASICON-structured NVPOF (PDF #01-076-3645) without any obvious impurity peaks. Moreover, the diffraction peaks have a high relative intensity, which also proves that the prepared NVPOF sample has an ideal crystal structure and good crystallinity. The FTIR spectrum of the prepared NVPOF is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, and the bands at 1000–1150, 667, and 557 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be ascribed to the asymmetric stretching vibration (<i>v</i><sub>as</sub>) of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup> tetrahedrons, the P–O symmetric stretching (<i>v</i><sub>ss</sub>), and bending vibrations (<i>v</i><sub>b</sub>), respectively. The weak band at 954 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the strong band at 913 cm<sup>–1</sup> indicate the presence of V–F and V–O stretching vibrations, respectively. Moreover, according to the FTIR response, the intensity difference originated from the different absorbance nature of chemical bonds, indicating fewer vanadium bonds with fluorine than with oxygen in the NVPOF lattice. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> In addition, the XPS measurement was performed to further determine the V valence. As shown in the full XPS spectrum of NVPOF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>), in addition to the matrix element Na, the presence of V, P, O, and F elements can also be observed. In the high-resolution V 2p spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>), the sharp peak centered at 516.5 eV corresponds to the electrons in the orbit of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, while another broad peak at a higher binding energy of 523.6 eV is attributed to the V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> electrons. Both peaks are characteristic of V<sup>4+</sup> species in NVPOF and are in good agreement with a previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural and morphological characterizations of NVPOF. (a) XRD pattern of the prepared sample and the corresponding standard card for NVPOF; (b) FTIR spectrum; and (c, d) SEM and low-resolution TEM images of the sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and the size of the prepared NVPOF were investigated by SEM and TEM, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d. NVPOF consists of uniform nanocuboids with a length of ∼400 nm and a width of ∼200 nm. The prepared NVPOF with uniform nanosize could effectively shorten the pathway for Na<sup>+</sup>, which is favorable for its diffusion during the charge/discharge process, indicating that NVPOF has an outstanding rate capacity, which is further investigated by electrochemical tests as given later. In addition, the elemental energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping of SEM in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> manifests the distribution of each element in NVPOF, indicating that all present elements Na, V, O, P, F, and C are uniformly distributed throughout the whole sample of NVPOF.</div><div class="NLM_p">To compare the effects of four different binders on the NVPOF material’s electrochemical characteristics, the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/intercalation processes of NVPOF with different binders at 2.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na were investigated by the common electrochemical measurement techniques, for example, the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) and cyclic voltammetry (CV). The GCD curves of the cells used for different binders (the electrode consisted of a mixture of NVPOF, carbon black, and the SA (the structural schematic diagram of the SA binder is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), CMC, PVDF, or LA133 binder and was denoted as NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, NVPOF-PVDF, or NVPOF-LA133, respectively) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. In all of the GCD curves, two pairs of charge/discharge plateaus can be clearly seen, which are attributed to the extraction/intercalation of two Na<sup>+</sup> in charge/discharge processes. The GCD curves of NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, and NVPOF-PVDF exhibit relatively high specific capacities of about 127, 122, and 122 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the first cycle. Although only a slight difference can be recognized between these three curves, obviously, NVPOF-SA has the best reversibility. As for NVPOF-PVDF, the capacity has obvious decay, which can be ascribed to the poor stability of the PVDF binder, specifically, the swelling of PVDF and the reduction of the viscosity during the charge/discharge processes in the ester-based electrolyte. NVPOF-LA133 exhibits the worst performance with the lowest discharge capacity and the poorest cycle stability during the charge/discharge processes. Moreover, CV tests were employed to illustrate the phase transformation and electrochemical reactions during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/intercalation processes in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, all of the CV profiles of these electrodes exhibit two coupled anodic/cathodic peaks, which are well-defined at 4.086/3.966 and 3.691/3.541 V for NVPOF-SA, 4.086/3.954 and 3.708/3.530 V for NVPOF-CMC, 4.089/3.926 and 3.727/3.425 V for NVPOF-PVDF, and 4.099/3.912 and 3.745/3.399 V for NVPOF-LA133, which coincided with the corresponding test results in GCD. Moreover, to further illustrate the influence of different binders on the polarization of the NVPOF electrodes, the polarization of the four electrodes based on the GCD and CV curves is shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. Obviously, NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC have smaller polarization compared with NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133, indicating that NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC have better electronic and ionic transport kinetics, which might have resulted from the higher electrical conductivity of the SA and CMC binders. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> In addition, it can be inferred that NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC exhibit a superior rate capacity, which is proven in the following.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) GCD curves of the NVPOF electrodes with four different binders at a rate of 0.1C. (b) CV curves of the NVPOF electrodes with four different binders at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Polarization of the Four Electrodes Calculated by the GCD and CV Curves</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" rowspan="2" scope="col" align="center">&nbsp;</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="4" scope="col" align="center" char=".">polarization (V)</th></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="2" scope="col" align="center" char=".">GCD curve</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="2" scope="col" align="center" char=".">CV curve</th></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">electrodes</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">high voltage</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">low voltage</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">high voltage</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">low voltage</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NVPOF-SA</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.021</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.022</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.127</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.155</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NVPOF-CMC</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.023</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.054</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.133</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.186</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NVPOF-PVDF</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.065</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.081</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.164</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.302</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NVPOF-LA133</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.056</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.125</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.198</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.352</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To further demonstrate the influence of different binders on the electrochemical performance of NVPOF, the rate performance and cycle stability were tested. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the rate performances of the NVPOF electrodes from 0.1 to 20C using different binders. NVPOF-SA shows the best rate capability and can deliver a specific capacity of about 127 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C, and even at 20C, it can still deliver a specific capacity of about 81 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which corresponds to a capacity retention of about 64.8% compared to the specific capacity at 0.1C. Moreover, there are two clear discharge plateaus above 3.6 and 4.0 V even at a high rate of 20C (in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). This is due to the formation of CEI at the surface between the cathode and the electrolyte, which provides better adhesion in the case of the NVPOF-SA electrode. NVPOF-CMC also has a great rate performance, which is close to that of NVPOF-SA. Furthermore, when the rate goes back to 0.1C, the discharge capacity can also go back to the initial one for both NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC, indicating that the electrodes prepared using SA and CMC as binders exhibit excellent rate capacity and stability. Compared with the NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC electrodes, the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes exhibit poorer rate performances, which do not even deliver any capacity at a rate of 15C. In addition to the rate performance, the cycling stabilities of the four electrodes were also investigated at a rate of 1C. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, NVPOF-SA exhibits excellent cycling stability with nearly no obvious capacity fading after 200 cycles and the Coulombic efficiency of the electrode is close to 100%, indicating superior stability and cohesiveness of the SA binder. While the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes show evident capacity fading in cycling processes, especially NVPOF-LA133, which delivers nearly no capacity after about 80 cycles. The GCD curves of the four electrodes at different cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, and the NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC electrodes demonstrate almost the same GCD curves under different cycles both on the platform and specific discharge capacity. However, it can be observed that the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes have obvious variation, including significant polarization and capacity attenuation with the increase of the number of cycles, especially the NVPOF-LA133 electrode. This is mainly due to the good contact between the electrode material and the current collector in the NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes and the good integrity of electrodes after cycling, leading to fast kinetic properties. Unsatisfactory results are observed for the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes, which are due to the swelling of PVDF in esters solvent and the strong adhesion of LA133, leading to an evident crack and agglomeration after cycling, as well as the inferior electrochemical performance of the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes. To further verify the stability of different binders on the electrochemical performance of NVPOF, we conducted long cycling tests at a relatively high current density (5C, whose charging/discharging time is only 0.2 h), and the specific result is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, indicating that the NVPOF-SA electrode exhibits the highest capacity and the best stability during charge/discharge processes. However, the electrodes using PVDF and LA133 as binders all show notably low capacity at a rate of 5C. The test results show that the NVPOF-SA electrode shows the best performance, which can be ascribed to the better adhesion and cohesion of the SA binder to a great extent, protecting the integrity of the electrode during the cycling processes, thereby improving the electrochemical performance of NVPOF, and this will be verified in the following. Additionally, NVPOF-SA exhibits remarkable electrochemical properties comparable to those of previously reported phosphate-based cathode materials for SIBs, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting Information.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders. (a) Rate capacity; (b) GCD curves at different rates; (c) cycling stability at 1C after 200 cycles; (d) GCD curves at different cycles; and (e) cycling stability at 5C after 600 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To shed light on the reasons for the influence of different binders on the electrochemical performance of the NVPOF electrodes, SEM tests were used to analyze the composition and architecture by characterizing the electrode in the fresh state/after cycling, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4a</a> are the SEM images of fresh NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC, respectively, in which the two electrodes show a relatively uniform distribution without any obvious agglomeration or crack, meaning that the SA/CMC binder can be in good contact with the electrode material and the current collector. Meanwhile, in NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC, the active material NVPOF and carbon black have a good contact, which can effectively improve the electrical conductivity of the whole electrode. Furthermore, a uniform distribution without agglomeration is beneficial to infiltrate the electrolyte, leading to a better contact between the active materials and the electrolyte during charge/discharge processes. The SEM image of the fresh NVPOF-PVDF is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c</a>, in which an obvious aggregation can be clearly seen, showing a non-uniform distribution of the electrode materials on the current collector. The poor contact between the active materials and the electrolyte leads to a high interface impedance and worse electrochemical performance. The aggregation is even more severe when using LA133 as the binder and the result is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, which shows a large crack due to the strong adhesion of LA133, meaning a worse contact between the electrode materials and the current collector, which hinders the diffusion and transport of Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons in the electrode. In addition to the fresh electrode, the composition and architecture of the electrodes after 200 cycles at 1C were also studied by SEM. Compared with the fresh ones, the cycled NVPOF-SA electrode displays slight agglomeration, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, indicating that the electrode is relatively stable when using SA as the binder. While, the NVPOF-CMC electrode appears to have a slight crack and agglomeration (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>), leading to the fact that the electrochemical performance of the NVPOF-CMC electrode is slightly lower than that of NVPOF-SA. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4d</a>, when using PVDF as the binder, an obvious agglomeration can be seen, which may be caused by the swelling and softening of PVDF in the ester-based electrolyte, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> indicating that PVDF could not provide enough cohesiveness during long-term cycling processes. Moreover, an evident crack and agglomeration can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, meaning that the LA133 binder is also not suitable for NVPOF. Furthermore, the adhesion strength of different binders between the electrode materials and the current collector was also tested by stress testing under a pressure of 10 MPa, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. It can be seen that the NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC electrodes show nearly no breakage after the pressure test, while the NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes exhibit different degrees of damage, further evidencing that the SA and CMC binders have good cohesiveness. In addition, the schematic diagram of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders during cycling processes is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. NVPOF with the SA binder can best maintain the integrity of the electrode after cycling, due to the high adhesive and cohesive properties of the SA binder, which can remarkably improve the electrochemical performance of the NVPOF electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. SEM images of the NVPOF electrode using different binders in the fresh state (a, c) and after 200 cycles at 1C (b, d). (a, b) NVPOF-SA and (c, d) NVPOF-LA133. (e) Schematic diagram of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders during cycling processes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further clarify the causes of the effects of different binders on the electrochemical performance of the NVPOF electrodes, the XPS test was conducted to analyze the adhesion and cohesion of different binders. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows, from top to bottom, the C 1s spectra of the fresh NVPOF electrodes using SA, CMC, PVDF, and LA133 as binders, respectively. From the spectra of NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, and NVPOF-LA133 electrodes, the binding energy at around 288.7, 286.7, and 285.4 eV can be attributed to the −CO<sub>2</sub>–, −CO–, and −CH<sub>2</sub>– of these aqueous binders, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref47" aria-label="reference 36 and 47">(36,47)</a> As for the NVPOF-PVDF electrode, the peaks at 290.7 and 286.0 eV correspond to the −CF<sub>2</sub>– and −CH<sub>2</sub>– of PVDF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The binding energy at 284.5 eV corresponds to the C–C bond of the carbon black in the four electrodes. After 200 cycles, the spectra of NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-LA133 show a new peak at around 288.0 eV, which can be attributed to the −CO<sub>2</sub>– of the electrolyte decomposition products containing an ester linkage. The peak at 283.2 eV in NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, and NVPOF-LA133 can be attributed to the −C═C– of the electrolyte decomposition products containing an unsaturated carbon bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The rich carboxyl and hydroxyl groups of the SA binder endow it with higher adhesive and cohesive properties, and the −C═C– bonds formed by the electrolyte reaction on the surface of the NVPOF-SA electrode can prevent the further decomposition of the electrolyte and the corrosion of the cathode, which effectively protect the integrity of the electrodes and thus ensure outstanding cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 49 to 52">(49−52)</a> The kinetic characteristics of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders were further studied by the EIS and GITT tests. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the impedance plots of the NVPOF electrodes using different binders are composed of a semicircle in the region of high to medium frequencies and a straight line in the low-frequency region. The semicircle of the high and medium frequencies represents the contact resistance (<i>R</i><sub><i>i</i></sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values can be obtained by fitting the EIS curves, and the fitting results are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, and NVPOF-PVDF electrodes have relatively small resistances (below 300 Ω), and the resistance of NVPOF-SA is only about 180 Ω, which can favor the electronic transfer processes and realize a small polarization. On the contrary, the NVPOF-LA133 electrode shows the largest resistance, indicating that the electronic conductivity of the NVPOF-LA133 electrode is worst compared with the other three electrodes. Moreover, the apparent diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) is associated with the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), which is reflected in the inclined line in the low-frequency region, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> can be calculated by <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons per molecule during oxidization, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, <i>C</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration, and σ is the Warburg factor, which is related to <i>Z</i>′ through<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) XPS spectra of the fresh NVPOF electrodes with different binders. (b) XPS spectra of the after-cycled NVPOF electrodes with different binders. (c) Nyquist plots of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders. (d) Variations and fittings between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region of the NVPOF electrodes with different binders.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the relationship between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region of the four electrodes with different binders. According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, the slope of the straight line has an inverse relation with <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>. So, it is clear that the electrode using SA as the binder has the maximum <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>, and the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of NVPOF-CMC is close to that of NVPOF-SA, while the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values of NVPOF-PVDF and NVPOF-LA133 are obviously smaller than those of NVPOF-SA and NVPOF-CMC. Usually, <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> has a great influence on the rate capacity of the materials, and a larger <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes will be easier, thus realizing an excellent electrochemical performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the GITT tests were conducted to track the changes in <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> during a complete charge and discharge process of the NVPOF cathode materials. The GITT tests for the first cycle were implemented in the same voltage range as that for GCD at 0.05C. There is a straight-line behavior between the cell voltage <i>E</i> and the square root of the galvanostatic time (τ<sup>1/2</sup>) during each single titration, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6–S9</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the trend of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) and quasi-equilibrium potentials in each relaxation process consistent with the GCD processes of the NVPOF electrodes using different binders. Notably, every quasi-equilibrium potential almost coincides with the charge and discharge curves, and they exhibit the minimum difference values compared to the previously reported results. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> That is to say, the cells effectively elevated both ionic and electronic conductivities during the Na<sup>+</sup> kinetic processes through binder regulation. According to the GITT testing result, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values of the electrodes with different binders were 10<sup>–12</sup>–10<sup>–9</sup>, 10<sup>–13</sup>–10<sup>–9</sup>, 10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–11</sup>, and 10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for NVPOF-SA, NVPOF-CMC, NVPOF-PVDF, and NVPOF-LA133, respectively, indicating that different binders have a significant effect on the kinetic properties of the NVPOF electrode, thus affecting the rate performance of the NVPOF materials.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. GITT test results: the trend of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) and quasi-equilibrium potentials in each relaxation process consistent with the GCD processes of the NVPOF electrodes using different binders. (a) NVPOF-SA; (b) NVPOF-CMC; (c) NVPOF-PVDF; and (d) NVPOF-LA133.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, SIFBs were fabricated via coupling the NVPOF-SA cathode with the CC anode, demonstrating the suitability of the chosen NVPOF-SA as a good material for the SIB cathode. The CC anode was prepared according to a previous report, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> and its structure and morphology were characterized by XRD and SEM, respectively. The XRD pattern of the CC anode shows two visible broad peaks at 20 and 43° (in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a</a>), corresponding to the (002) and (100) crystal planes of the disordered carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> respectively. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>, the CC anode is composed of interwoven carbon microtubes (the diameter is approximately 10–15 μm), and the representative electrochemical properties of the CC anode are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a shows the first cycle curves of the CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs at 0.1C (calculated based on the NVPOF cathode) in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3 V, in which two well-defined plateaus at about 3.5 and 4.0 V can be clearly recognized, indicating a good match between these two electrodes. In addition, the discharge specific capacity of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs in the first cycle is as high as 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, while the Coulombic efficiency of the first cycle is 92.3%. Compared with the half-cell test, the discharge specific capacity and the Coulombic efficiency of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs are lower, which is mainly due to the fact that the full cell undergoes the activation process in the first cycle. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a further shows the application demonstrations of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs in a coin-type cell. As shown in the digital photo, one coin-type cell can light a display screen composed of 33 LED bulbs. Moreover, the rate performance of the full cell was also tested from 0.1C to 15C and is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b, and the discharge capacity at 0.1C is overall greater than 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is almost the same as that of the half-cells. Even when the rate is increased to 15C, the full cell can still deliver a capacity of about 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, owing to a capacity retention of 75% compared with the specific capacity at 0.1C. The results indicate that the SA binder is not only suitable for half-cells but also for full cells, which are prepared by coupling NVPOF-SA and CC and therefore have excellent electrochemical performance.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/medium/am0c14294_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) First cycle curves of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs and the practical application of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs (inset). (b) Rate capacity of CC//NVPOF-SA SIFBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/asset/images/large/am0c14294_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14294&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we have proved that the electrochemical performance of NVPOF cathode materials can be improved by matching the appropriate binder system and further studied the effects of different binders on the electrochemical properties of NVPOF. On the whole, the NVPOF electrodes with the aqueous SA binder exhibit the best electrochemical performance compared with those with other binders, including excellent rate capacity (a capacity of 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 20C) and stable cycling performance (a capacity retention of 89.2% after 600 cycles at 5C), which is attributed to the excellent adhesion of SA (ensuring the integrity of the electrode in the cycling process), thereby reducing the polarization and improving the kinetic properties of the NVPOF electrode. Furthermore, by coupling the NVPOF-SA cathode with the CC anode, a full cell battery was assembled, which can also achieve an excellent performance. The strategy of matching the appropriate binder will become a new breakthrough point for developing electrode materials with high performance for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14294?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14294</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of the NVPOF electrode using different binders in the fresh state and after 200 cycles at 1C; pressure test for the NVPOF electrodes; GITT profiles of every titration period for the NVPOF cathode; electrochemical characteristics for the CC anode; electrochemical performance comparison (Table S1); and impedance parameters (Table S2) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf">am0c14294_si_001.pdf (1.25 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c14294" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoscale design to enable the revolution in renewable energy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Baxter, Jason; Bian, Zhixi; Chen, Gang; Danielson, David; Dresselhaus, Mildred S.; Fedorov, Andrei G.; Fisher, Timothy S.; Jones, Christopher W.; Maginn, Edward; Kortshagen, Uwe; Manthiram, Arumugam; Nozik, Arthur; Rolison, Debra R.; Sands, Timothy; Shi, Li; Sholl, David; Wu, Yiying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">559-588</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The creation of a sustainable energy generation, storage, and distribution infrastructure represents a global grand challenge that requires massive transnational investments in the research and development of energy technologies that will provide the amt. of energy needed on a sufficient scale and timeframe with minimal impact on the environment and have limited economic and societal disruption during implementation.  In this opinion paper, we focus on an important set of solar, thermal, and electrochem. energy conversion, storage, and conservation technologies specifically related to recent and prospective advances in nanoscale science and technol. that offer high potential in addressing the energy challenge.  We approach this task from a two-fold perspective: analyzing the fundamental physicochem. principles and engineering aspects of these energy technologies and identifying unique opportunities enabled by nanoscale design of materials, processes, and systems in order to improve performance and reduce costs.  Our principal goal is to establish a roadmap for research and development activities in nanoscale science and technol. that would significantly advance and accelerate the implementation of renewable energy technologies.  In all cases we make specific recommendations for research needs in the near-term (2-5 years), mid-term (5-10 years) and long-term (&gt; 10 years), as well as projecting a timeline for maturation of each technol. soln.  We also identify a no. of priority themes in basic energy science that cut across the entire spectrum.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.2085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Larcher, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01388b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium-ion batteries. A look into the future</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">3287</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c1ee01388b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D4%26publication_year%3D2011%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DB.%2BScrosati%26author%3DJ.%2BHassoun%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DLithium-ion%2Bbatteries.%2BA%2Blook%2Binto%2Bthe%2Bfuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc1ee01388b&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c14294&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc1ee01388b&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-ion batteries. A look into the future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Scrosati, Bruno; Hassoun, Jusef; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3287-3295</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  A crit. overview of the latest developments in the lithium ion batteries technol. is reported.  We first describe the evolution in the electrolyte area with particular attention to ionic liqs., discussing the expected application of these room temp. molten salts and listing the issues that still prevent their practical implementation.  The attention is then focused on the electrode materials presently considered the most promising for enhancing the energy d. of the batteries.  At the anode side a discussion is provided on the status of development of high capacity tin and silicon lithium alloys.  We show that the morphol. that is the most likely to ensure com. exploitation of these alloy electrodes is that involving carbon-based nanocomposites.  We finally touch on super-high-capacity batteries, discussing the key cases of lithium-sulfur and lithium-air and attempting to forecast their chances to eventually reach the status of practically appealing energy storage systems.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources. The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoflake-constructed porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C hierarchical microspheres as a bicontinuous cathode for sodium-ion batteries applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Xinxin; Pan, Anqiang; Yin, Bo; Fang, Guozhao; Wang, Yaping; Kong, Xiangzhong; Zhu, Ting; Zhou, Jiang; Cao, Guozhong; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">60</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention for large-scale energy storage systems as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the huge availability and low-cost.  Yet the development of SIBs has been hindered by the low reversibility, sluggish ion diffusion, as well as large vol. variations.  Herein, we report an efficient hydrothermal method for fabricating hierarchical porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) microspheres assembled from interconnected nanoflakes.  The NVP nanocrystals are uniformly wrapped by N-doped carbon layer.  As a half-cell cathode, the NVP/C porous microspheres exhibit superior rate capability (99.3 mA h g-1 at 100 C) and excellent cyclic stability (79.1% capacity retention over 10,000 cycles at 20 C).  A full-cell configuration coupled with NVP/C cathode and SnS/C fibers anode exhibits an estd. practical energy d. of 223 W h kg-1.  The superior performance can be ascribed to the hierarchical porous micro/nano structure along with N-doped carbon encapsulation, which provide bicontinuous electron/ion pathways, large electrode-electrolyte contact area, as well as robust structural integrity.  This work provides a promising approach for boosting the electrochem. performance of battery materials via the integration of hierarchical structure and heteroatoms doped carbon coating.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201502583"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Superior Na3V2(PO4)3-Based Nanocomposite Enhanced by Both N-Doped Coating Carbon and Graphene as the Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Jin-Zhi; Wu, Xing-Long; Wan, Fang; Wang, Jie; Zhang, Xiao-Hua; Wang, Rong-Shun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A superior Na3V2(PO4)3-based nanocomposite (NVP/C/rGO) was successfully developed by a facile carbothermal redn. method using one most-common chelator, disodium ethylenediamintetraacetate [Na2(C10H16N2O8)], as both Na and N-doped C sources for the 1st time. 2-dimensional-reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nanosheets are also employed as highly conductive additives to facilitate the elec. cond. and limit the growth of NVP nanoparticles.  When used as the cathode material for Na-ion batteries, the NVP/C/rGO nanocomposite exhibits the highest discharge capacity, the best high-rate capabilities and prolonged cycling life compared to the pristine NVP and single-C-modified NVP/C.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Caging Na3V2(PO4)2F3 Microcubes in Cross-Linked Graphene Enabling Ultrafast Sodium Storage and Long-Term Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cai Yangsheng; Cao Xinxin; Luo Zhigao; Fang Guozhao; Liu Fei; Zhou Jiang; Pan Anqiang; Liang Shuquan; Zhou Jiang; Pan Anqiang; Liang Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are widely regarded as a promising supplement for lithium-ion battery technology.  However, it still suffers from some challenges, including low energy/power density and unsatisfactory cycling stability.  Here, a cross-linked graphene-caged Na3V2(PO4)2F3 microcubes (NVPF@rGO) composite via a one-pot hydrothermal strategy followed by freeze drying and heat treatment is reported.  As a cathode for a sodium-ion half-cell, the NVPF@rGO delivers excellent cycling stability and rate capability, as well as good low temperature adaptability.  The structural evolution during the repeated Na(+) extraction/insertion and Na ions diffusion kinetics in the NVPF@rGO electrode are investigated.  Importantly, a practicable sodium-ion full-cell is constructed using a NVPF@rGO cathode and a N-doped carbon anode, which delivers outstanding cycling stability (95.1% capacity retention over 400 cycles at 10 C), as well as an exceptionally high energy density (291 Wh kg(-1) at power density of 192 W kg(-1)).                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The electrochemical exploration of double carbon-wrapped Na3V2(PO4)3: Towards long-time cycling and superior rate sodium-ion battery cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Sijie; Ge, Peng; Zhang, Chenyang; Sun, Wei; Hou, Hongshuai; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
