        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) is a very promising cathode material in sodium ion battery for rapidly emerging large-scale energy storage with its classical 3D NASCION structure.  However, the cycling life and rate performances are restricted its low electronic cond.  To overcome these, the double carbon-wrapped Na3V2(PO4)3 composite is firstly designed through rheol. phase approach, delivering enhanced electrochem. properties.  The unique double carbon layers are composed of uniform amorphous carbons as protecting framework for stabilizing the structure, as well as the graphitized carbon sheets playing the role of conductive network for better electronic cond.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing need to store an increasing amt. of renewable energy in a sustainable way has rekindled interest for sodium-ion battery technol., owing to the natural abundance of sodium.  Presently, sodium-ion batteries based on Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C are the subject of intense research focused on improving the energy d. by harnessing the third sodium, which has so far been reported to be electrochem. inaccessible.  Here, we are able to trigger the activity of the third sodium electrochem. via the formation of a disordered NaxV2(PO4)2F3 phase of tetragonal symmetry (I4/mmm space group). However, present methods for prepn. of Na3V2(PO4)2F3 suffer from either high energy consumption or generating poor rate performance.  Herein, a cost-effective solvothermal-ball-milling method is proposed to solve the problem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In the search for new pos.-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, recent research has focused on nanostructured lithium transition-metal phosphates that exhibit desirable properties such as high energy storage capacity combined with electrochem. stability.  Only one member of this class-the olivine LiFePO4 (ref. 3)-has risen to prominence so far, owing to its other characteristics, which include low cost, low environmental impact and safety.  These are crit. for large-capacity systems such as plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles.  Nonetheless, olivine has some inherent shortcomings, including 1-dimensional lithium-ion transport and a two-phase redox reaction that together limit the mobility of the phase boundary.  Thus, nanocrystallites are key to enable fast rate behavior.  Also probably the long-term economic viability of large-scale Li-ion energy storage systems could be ultimately limited by global lithium reserves, although this remains speculative at present.  Here, the authors report on a sodium/lithium iron phosphate, A2FePO4F (A = Na, Li), that could serve as a cathode in either Li-ion or Na-ion cells.  Also, it possesses facile two-dimensional pathways for Li+ transport, and the structural changes on redn.-oxidn. are minimal.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-rate capability of Na2FePO4F nanoparticles by enhancing surface carbon functionality for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ko, Jesse S.; Doan-Nguyen, Vicky V. T.; Kim, Hyung-Seok; Petrissans, Xavier; De Block, Ryan H.; Choi, Christopher S.; Long, Jeffrey W.; Dunn, Bruce S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal phosphate compds. are considered promising candidates as pos. electrode materials for Na-ion batteries because they offer higher cation-insertion potentials than analogous metal oxides.  One such example is sodium iron fluorophosphate (Na2FePO4F), a compd. that is typically synthesized by high-temp. solid-state routes.  In this study, phase-pure Na2FePO4F is prepd. using the polyol route, a low-temp. process that allows for the synthesis of nanoparticles (15-25 nm), a form that enhances Na-ion insertion kinetics and cycling stability.  Two methods are applied to enhance the electronic cond. of Na2FePO4F: (i) converting residual org. byproducts of the polyol synthesis to conductive carbon coatings and (ii) prepg. a nanocomposite with reduced graphene oxide.  The resulting electrode materials are characterized in nonaq. Na-ion electrolytes, assessing such metrics as specific capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability.  A thorough electrochem. kinetics anal. is performed to deconvolve surface-vs.-bulk Na-ion insertion as a function of composite structure.  Specific capacities between 60-110 mA h g-1 were achieved in galvanostatic charge-discharge tests when cycling in the range from 10C to C/10, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b02539"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smiley, D. R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ex Situ 23Na Solid-State NMR Reveals the Local Na-Ion Distribution in Carbon-Coated Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F during Electrochemical Cycling</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ex Situ23Na Solid-State NMR Reveals the Local Na-Ion Distribution in Carbon-Coated Na2FePO4F during Electrochemical Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Smiley, Danielle L.; Goward, Gillian R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The potential Na-ion cathode material Na2FePO4F was studied here by ex situ23Na solid-state NMR (ssNMR) to characterize the structure and ion mobility as a function of electrochem. cycling.  The use of fast magic angle spinning (MAS) speeds of 65 kHz allows for the collection of high-resoln. 23Na NMR spectra that reveal two unique peaks at +450 and -175 ppm, corresponding to the two crystallog. unique Na sites in the material of interest.  Two-dimensional NMR exchange spectroscopy results reveal that chem. exchange between the Na ions residing in distinct environments has a max. hopping rate of ∼200 Hz.  The collection of 1-dimensional NMR spectra as a function of electrochem. cycling reveals the reproducible formation of a new peak at +320 ppm in the 23Na NMR spectrum at all intermediate states of charge.  The appearance of this resonance at +320 ppm is attributed to the fully oxidized (NaFePO4F) phase that is present even upon initial electrochem. oxidn.  The simultaneous existence of both the pristine and oxidized phases suggest formation of two distinct phases upon charging, consistent with a two-phase desodiation mechanism.  This two-phase arrangement of Na ions persists for multiple charge/discharge cycles and is congruent with high reversibility of Na (de)intercalation in Na2FePO4F cathodes.  These findings imply that the Na2FePO4F framework is incredibly structurally stable with a robust intercalation process, despite a lack of ideal Na-ion kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1149/1.1523691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barker, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saidi, M.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A sodium-ion cell based on the fluorophosphate compound NaVPO4F</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barker, J.; Saidi, M. Y.; Swoyer, J. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical and Solid-State Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Preliminary performance data is presented for a novel sodium-ion cell based on a hard carbon/NaVPO4F chem.  To our knowledge this is the first time that a viable sodium-ion system based on a polyanion cathode material has been demonstrated.  The NaVPO4F was prepd. by a simple incorporation reaction involving vanadium(III) phosphate, VPO4 and NaF.  The hard carbon was from a com. source.  Const. current cycling indicated reversible alkali-ion insertion behavior for the fluorophosphate material, while the generally sym. nature of the charge/discharge curves indicated the energetic reversibility of the insertion system.  The av. discharge voltage for the sodium-ion cells was demonstrated to around 3.7 V, a figure comparable with com. available lithium-ion cells.  The reversible specific capacities for the cathode and anode active materials were detd. to be 82 and 202 mAh/g, resp.  Representative test cells cycled more than 30 times before their discharge capacity had declined to less then 50% of the original.  The relatively high first cycle charge inefficiency is thought to be due to irreversible passivation reactions occurring at the carbon surface.  Significant performance enhancements may be made by optimization of the electrolyte system and improving the active material mass balance.  In summary, low overvoltage, good insertion reversibility, and reasonable capacity retention characterize this fluorophosphate-based sodium-ion cell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700087"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F/C Nanofibers as Self-Standing Cathode Material for Ultralong Cycle Life Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Precisely controlled preparation of an advanced Na3V2(PO4)2O2F cathode material for sodium ion batteries: the optimization of electrochemical properties and electrode kinetics</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Zhen-Yi; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Yang, Yang; Yu, Hai-Yue; Xi, Xiao-Tong; Zhao, Xin-Xin; Guan, Hong-Yu; He, Xiaoyan; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry Frontiers</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the high working voltage and energy-storage d. and excellent cycling stability, Na3V2(PO4)2O2F (NVPOF) has been recognized as a promising cathode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Usually, NVPOF is prepd. by a high-temp. solid-state method, which easily leads to many deficiencies including large particle size, irregular morphol. and poor high-rate capability.  In order to avoid these deficiencies, the present study reveals a simple hydrothermal method to effectively regulate the morphol. and size uniformity of the NVPOF particles and optimize the electrochem. properties by precisely adjusting the key prepn. parameters including pH and temp.  It is disclosed that the optimized NVPOF prepd. at pH = 7.00 ± 0.05 and 170 °C can achieve outstanding electrochem. properties in terms of high specific capacity of 123.2 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C, superior high-rate capability of 85.9 mA h g-1 at 20 C and long cycling stability (e.g., a capacity retention up to 94.8% at 1 C after 500 cycles).  All of the properties are obviously better than those of the controls obtained at other prepn. parameters.  The much enhanced performance should be beneficial from the short and fast Na+ transport pathways because of the high uniformity and nanometer size of the NVPOF particles, making them a promising cathode material for high-performance SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201701968"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J. Ramesh; Jung, Young Hwa; Wang, Ji Eun; Kim, Do Kyung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

As a cathode material for non-aq. sodium-ion batteries, as-synthesized Na3V2O2(PO4)2F-MWCNT composite shows stable capacity of 98 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C for 120 cycles and 60 mAh g-1 at 2 C for 1800 cycles in half-cell and full-cell configurations, resp.  In aq. electrolytes, Na3V2O2(PO4)2F-MWCNT composite delivers discharge capacity of 35 mAh g-1 at 1 C rate in half-cell and 42 mAh g-1 at 1 C rate in full-cell with NaTi2(PO4)3-MWCNT as an anode.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Quasi-Solid-State Sodium-Ion Full Battery with High-Power/Energy Densities</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Jin-Zhi; Yang, Ai-Bo; Gu, Zhen-Yi; Wu, Xing-Long; Pang, Wei-Lin; Ning, Qiu-Li; Li, Wen-Hao; Zhang, Jing-Ping; Su, Zhong-Min</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing a high-performance, low-cost, and safer rechargeable battery is a primary challenge in next-generation electrochem. energy storage.  Here, a quasi-solid-state (QSS) Na-ion full battery (SIFB) is designed and fabricated.  Hard C cloth derived from cotton cloth and Na3V2(PO4)2O2F (NVPOF) are used as the anode and the cathode, resp., and a Na ion-conducting gel-polymer membrane is used as both the QSS electrolyte and separator, accomplishing the high energy and power densities in the QSS Na-ion batteries.  The energy d. can reach 460 Wh/Kg according to the mass of the cathode materials.  The fabricated QSS SIFB also exhibits an excellent rate performance (e.g., ∼78.1 mAh/g specific capacity at 10 C) and a superior cycle performance (e.g., ∼90% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 10 C).  These results show that the developed QSS SIFB is a hopeful candidate for large-scale energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201601925"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jian, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical Ru-doped sodium vanadium fluorophosphates hollow microspheres as a cathode of enhanced superior rate capability and ultralong stability for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Manhua; Zhang, Dongtang; Zheng, Limin; Wang, Xiayan; Lin, Yue; Xia, Dingguo; Sun, Yugang; Guo, Guangsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Novel hierarchical Ru-doped Na3V2O2(PO4)2F hollow microspheres were synthesized via a low-temp. solvothermal method.  The individual unique microspheres were formed from assembly of numerous nanoparticles with diams. of 20-30 nm.  When used as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the microspheres exhibited superior rate performance with a capacity of 72.6 mAh·g-1 at 10 C.  Furthermore, their rate performance could be significantly improved by coating them with a thin conductive RuO2 layer.  For instance, high specific capacities of 102.5 mAh g-1 and 44.9 mAh g-1 were achieved at current rates of 20 C and 100 C, resp.  These materials exhibited impressive long-term cycling stability.  A reversible capacity of approx. 55.0 mAh g-1 was maintained even after 7500 charge/discharge cycles.  D. functional theory (DFT) calcns. increased our understanding of how H+ facilitates the formation of the hierarchical microsphere superstructure which is beneficial to achieve a good rate capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.07.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubiak, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lezama, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced electrochemical performance of vanadyl (IV) Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F by ex-situ carbon coating</span>. <i>Electrochem.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced electrochemical performance of vanadyl (IV) Na3(VO)2(PO4)2F by ex-situ carbon coating</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Serras, Paula; Palomares, Veronica; Kubiak, Pierre; Lezama, Luis; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A vanadyl (IV) Na3(VO)2(PO4)2F compd. has been successfully synthesized by a novel single-step hydrothermal method.  ESR measurements have confirmed the existence of a V+4 phase.  This material has been carbon coated via an impregnation method followed by either a long thermal treatment (composite A) or a flash thermal treatment (composite B).  X-Ray diffraction patterns showed that composite B exhibits the presence of a pure Na3(VO)2(PO4)2F phase while a mixt. of Na3(VO)2(PO4)2F and Na3V2(PO4)3 appears in A.  The carbon content was 2.65 and 4.5 wt. % for A and B, resp.  The Na3(VO)2(PO4)2F material shows in all cases two voltage plateaux at 3.6 and 4.0 V vs. Na/Na+.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Moving to Aqueous Binder: A Valid Approach to Achieving High-Rate Capability and Long-Term Durability for Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao Jing; Yang Xu; Yao Ye; Gao Yu; Chen Gang; Du Fei; Sui Yongming; Zou Bo; Ehrenberg Helmut</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

However, a number of challenging issues remain regarding its rate capability and cycle life, which must be addressed to enable greater application compatibility.  Here, a facile and effective approach that can be used to overcome these disadvantages by introducing an aqueous carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder is reported.  The resulting conductive network serves to accelerate the diffusion of Na(+) ions across the interface as well as in the bulk.  The strong binding force of the CMC and stable solid permeable interface protect the electrode from degradation, leading to an excellent capacity of 75 mA h g(-1) at an ultrahigh rate of 70 C (1 C = 128 mA g(-1)) and a long lifespan of 3500 cycles at 30 C while sustaining 79% of the initial capacity value.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tremendous efforts have been devoted to the development of electrode materials, electrolytes, and separators of energy-storage devices to address the fundamental needs of emerging technologies such as elec. vehicles, artificial intelligence, and virtual reality.  However, binders, as an important component of energy-storage devices, are yet to receive similar attention.  Polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) has been the dominant binder in the battery industry for decades despite several well-recognized drawbacks, i.e., limited binding strength due to the lack of chem. bonds with electroactive materials, insufficient mech. properties, and low electronic and lithium-ion conductivities.  The limited binding function cannot meet inherent demands of emerging electrode materials with high capacities such as silicon anodes and sulfur cathodes.  To address these concerns, in this review we divide the binding between active materials and binders into two major mechanisms: mech. interlocking and interfacial binding forces.  We review existing and emerging binders, binding technol. used in energy-storage devices (including lithium-ion batteries, lithium-sulfur batteries, sodium-ion batteries, and supercapacitors), and state-of-the-art mech. characterization and computational methods for binder research.  Finally, we propose prospective next-generation binders for energy-storage devices from the mol. level to the macro level.  Functional binders will play crucial roles in future high-performance energy-storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1149/1.3526598"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, J.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Geiculescu, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">DesMarteau, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Creager, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionomer Binders Can Improve Discharge Rate Capability in Lithium-Ion Battery Cathodes</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ionomer Binders Can Improve Discharge Rate Capability in Lithium-Ion Battery Cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Oh, Jung-Min; Geiculescu, Olt; Des Marteau, Darryl; Creager, Stephen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

Cathodes were tested against Li4Ti5O12 anodes using 1.0 M and 0.1 M LiPF6-ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC) electrolytes.  Comparison was made with cathodes prepd. using poly(vinylidene) difluoride (PVDF) as binder.  At low discharge rates (e.g., C/5) both cathode types exhibited similar charge-discharge capacities and voltage profiles.  However, under higher rate discharge conditions (e.g., °1C, up to 5C) cathodes prepd. using ionomer binder showed better discharge rate capability than cathodes having PVDF binder.  This phenomenon was more pronounced when the salt concn. in the electrolyte was low (e.g., 0.1 M LiPF6-EC/DEC).  These findings suggest that use of ionic binders can help to compensate for electrolyte depletion from the electrode porous space as lithium ions are intercalated into lithium-deficient LiFePO4 particles during rapid discharging.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The effect of different binders on electrochemical properties of LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 cathode material in lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Jiantie; Chou, Shu-Lei; Gu, Qin-fen; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

NMC with uniform particle size at 100-200 nm.  The performance of the NMC electrodes with Na carboxylmethyl cellulose (CMC), poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF), and alginate from brown algae as binders was compared. Most electrodes are currently prepd. using poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) as binder.  This fluorinated polymer is expensive and requires the use of a volatile and toxic org. solvent such as N-methyl-pyrrolidone (NMP) in the processing.  Water sol. sodium CM-cellulose (CMC) can be a suitable substitute for PVDF as binder for both anodes and cathodes eliminating the necessity of NMP and thus decreasing the cost and the environmental impact of battery prodn. To solve the issues with water soly. and antibacterial stability of PVP-I2 (povidone-iodine) as a strong antibacterial agent, we successfully obtain hydrophobic povidone-iodine nanoparticles (povidone-iodine NPs) by a two-step method related to the advantage of nanotechnol. Interestingly, three products, such as glue, ink, and dye, after the incorporation of povidone-iodine NPs, show significant antibacterial properties.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Study on Polymer Binders for High-Capacity SiO Negative Electrode of Li-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Shimomura, Keiji; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Yui, Hiroharu; Konno, Kohzo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-capacity SiO powder composite electrodes for rechargeable Li-ion batteries are prepd. with different polymer binders of poly(acrylic acid) (PAA), poly(vinyl alc.) (PVA), Na CM-cellulose (CMCNa), and conventional poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF).  Electrode performance of the SiO composites depends on the binder, and their electrochem. reversibility is improved by using PAA as the binder in comparison to the PVdF, CMCNa, and PVA binders.  Coulombic efficiency at the initial cycle is improved for the SiO-PAA composite electrode, and the reversible capacity reaches 700-750 mA-h/g for continuous fifty cycling test at a rate of 100 mA/g.  The improvement mechanism of SiO-PAA composite electrode is characterized by x-ray diffraction, electron microscopy, XPS, IR spectroscopy, self-discharge test, and adhesive strength test.  Amorphous PAA polymer not only tightly binds but also covers the individual SiO particles.  Also, the PAA binder suppresses swelling of the composite electrode with the electrolyte soln. compared to the PVdF binder.  Through-thickness elec. resistance of the PAA composite electrode is much lower than that of the PVdF when it is wet with the electrolyte.  Probably these characters of the PAA binder effectively suppress isolation of the SiO powders in the composite electrode assocd. with the large vol. expansion/shrinkage during the lithiation/de-lithiation processes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2012.07.142"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moretti, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, G.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of different binding agents for nanocrystalline anatase TiO2 anodes and its application in a novel, green lithium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moretti, Arianna; Kim, Guk-Tae; Bresser, Dominic; Renger, Katrin; Paillard, Elie; Marassi, Roberto; Winter, Martin; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of nanocryst. anatase TiO2 electrodes made using sodium CM-cellulose (CMC) binder, alone and in combination with styrene butadiene rubber (SBR), was investigated.  The electrochem. behavior of these electrodes, which were made via a water-based process, was compared with those of electrodes manufd. using traditional polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) and its copolymer with hexafluoropropylene (PVDF-HFP) in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone soln.  Without the need of any post coating treatment, such as roll pressing, CMC and CMC/SBR based electrodes possess higher specific capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability than those prepd. using PVDF or PVDF-HFP as binding agent.  Using the same water based process, CMC based LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 (NMC) electrodes were prepd.  The combination of CMC based TiO2 and NMC electrodes in a lab.-scale lithium-ion full cell showed a stable cycling performance with a total energy d. of more than 120 Wh kg-1.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Plum pudding model inspired KVPO4F@3DC as high-voltage and hyperstable cathode for potassium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhaomeng; Wang, Jue; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science Bulletin</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The investigation on the cathode material of potassium ion batteries (PIBs), one of the most promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries, is of great significance.  Potassium vanadium fluorophosphate (KVPO4F) with a high working voltage is an appealing cathode candidate for PIBs, while the poor cycling performance and low electronic cond. dramatically hinder the application.  Herein, a plum pudding model inspired three-dimensional amorphous carbon network modified KVPO4F composite (KVPO4F@3DC) is successfully designed in this study to tackle these problems.  In the composite, KVPO4F particles are homogeneously wrapped by a layer of amorphous carbon and bridged by crosslinked large area carbon sheets.  As the cathode for PIBs, the KVPO4F@3DC composite exhibits a high av. operating voltage about 4.10 V with a super-high discharge capacity of 102.96 mAh g-1 at 20 mA g-1.  An excellent long cycle stability with a capacity retention of 85.4% over 550 cycles at 500 mA g-1 is achieved.  In addn., it maintains 83.6% of its initial capacity at 50 mA g-1 after 100 cycles at 55 °C.  The design of KVPO4F@3DC with plum pudding structure provides facilitative electron conductive network and stable electrode/electrode interface for electrode, successfully innovating an ultra-stable and high-performance cathode material for potassium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201804766"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Influence of electrolyte additives on the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) formation on LiNi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in half cells with Li metal counter electrode</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>329</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">31</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">40</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2016.08.023</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D329%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D31-40%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DY.%2BQian%26author%3DP.%2BNiehoff%26author%3DM.%2BB%25C3%25B6rner%26author%3DM.%2BGr%25C3%25BCtzke%26author%3DX.%2BM%25C3%25B6nnighoff%26author%3DP.%2BBehrends%26author%3DS.%2BNowak%26author%3DM.%2BWinter%26author%3DF.%2BM.%2BSchappacher%26title%3DInfluence%2Bof%2Belectrolyte%2Badditives%2Bon%2Bthe%2Bcathode%2Belectrolyte%2Binterphase%2B%2528CEI%2529%2Bformation%2Bon%2BLiNi1%252F3Mn1%252F3Co1%252F3O2%2Bin%2Bhalf%2Bcells%2Bwith%2BLi%2Bmetal%2Bcounter%2Belectrode%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2016.08.023&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c14294&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2016.08.023&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Influence of electrolyte additives on the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) formation on LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 in half cells with Li metal counter electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qian, Yunxian; Niehoff, Philip; Boerner, Markus; Gruetzke, Martin; Moennighoff, Xaver; Behrends, Pascal; Nowak, Sascha; Winter, Martin; Schappacher, Falko M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">329</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">31-40</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Traditional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) forming additives of vinylene carbonate (VC), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and ethylene sulfite (ES) are studied with respect to their impact on the formation and growth of the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) layer.  T-half cells are assembled and undergo three different electrochem. study plans: after formation (0.1 C, 5 cycles) and long term cycling (0.1 C, 5 const. current cycles + 1C, 100/150 const. current/voltage cycles), SEM, XRD, XPS and gas chromatog.-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) are combined to study morphol., CEI compn., CEI thickness and aging products for cells with different electrolyte systems.  The obtained results reveal a significant influence of these additives on the CEI compn. and CEI growth.  With the help of SEM, large areas of electrolyte decompn. products are formed at the aged electrode surfaces ( = after cycling), with the exception when 2 vol.% of FEC is added into the ref. electrolyte.  From XPS measurements, CEI thicknesses are calcd.  The ref. electrolyte with 2 vol.% of FEC shows the thinnest layer after long time aging (0.8 ± 0.2 nm).  For the addn. of 2 vol.% of VC, an incremental growth of the CEI thickness occurs from the 100th to 150th cycle (from 1.0 ± 0.1 nm to 2.9 ± 0.4 nm).  By correlating the CEI thickness values with the electrochem. performance, for lithium metal based half cells, the existence of a thinner CEI layer corresponds to a better cycling behavior, with 2 vol.% of FEC showing the highest discharge capacity of 114.4 ± 0.2 mAh/g after 150 cycles at 1C.  GC-MS shows that both VC and FEC help to prevent fast electrolyte aging.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.124407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rapidly synthesizing interconnected carbon nanocage by microwave toward high-performance aluminum batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>389</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">124407</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2020.124407</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D389%26publication_year%3D2020%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DE.%2BZhang%26author%3DB.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DH.%2BDing%26author%3DS.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BDuan%26author%3DX.%2BYu%26author%3DB.%2BLu%26title%3DRapidly%2Bsynthesizing%2Binterconnected%2Bcarbon%2Bnanocage%2Bby%2Bmicrowave%2Btoward%2Bhigh-performance%2Baluminum%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2020.124407&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c14294&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2020.124407&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rapidly synthesizing interconnected carbon nanocage by microwave toward high-performance aluminum batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Erjin; Wang, Bin; Wang, Jue; Ding, Hongbo; Zhang, Shi; Duan, Huigao; Yu, Xinzhi; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">389</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">124407</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cathode materials with specific physicochem. properties are key to the excellent electrochem. performance of aluminum batteries.  Here, we propose a catalyst-free microwave pulse radiation method to simply and rapidly convert Ketjen black into inter-connected carbon nanocage.  The product has a three-dimensional inter-connected network framework, a large sp. surface area, and eximious structural stability, which is beneficial for industrial development applications.  Besides, mol. dynamics simulations show that when inter-connected carbon nanocage is used to AlCl4- anion storage, it has a self-protection mechanism, which can avoid structure collapse caused by excessive anion embedding.  The inter-connected carbon nanocage exhibits excellent discharge capacity of 117 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 1000 mA g-1 and exceptional stability with a discharge capacity as high as 105 mAh g-1 at 2000 mA g-1 after 1000 cycles as the cathode for the aluminum battery.  The study greatly increases the prodn. speed and reduces the requirements of exptl. conditions for carbon nanocage, which may lead to a significant breakthrough for rapid, large-scale prodn. of aluminum battery cathode materials and other fields.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318805cccd854b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51'],'ref52':['cit52'],'ref53':['cit53']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

                10.1039/b821698c</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoscale design to enable the revolution in renewable energy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Baxter, Jason; Bian, Zhixi; Chen, Gang; Danielson, David; Dresselhaus, Mildred S.; Fedorov, Andrei G.; Fisher, Timothy S.; Jones, Christopher W.; Maginn, Edward; Kortshagen, Uwe; Manthiram, Arumugam; Nozik, Arthur; Rolison, Debra R.; Sands, Timothy; Shi, Li; Sholl, David; Wu, Yiying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">559-588</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The creation of a sustainable energy generation, storage, and distribution infrastructure represents a global grand challenge that requires massive transnational investments in the research and development of energy technologies that will provide the amt. of energy needed on a sufficient scale and timeframe with minimal impact on the environment and have limited economic and societal disruption during implementation.  In this opinion paper, we focus on an important set of solar, thermal, and electrochem. energy conversion, storage, and conservation technologies specifically related to recent and prospective advances in nanoscale science and technol. that offer high potential in addressing the energy challenge.  We approach this task from a two-fold perspective: analyzing the fundamental physicochem. principles and engineering aspects of these energy technologies and identifying unique opportunities enabled by nanoscale design of materials, processes, and systems in order to improve performance and reduce costs.  Our principal goal is to establish a roadmap for research and development activities in nanoscale science and technol. that would significantly advance and accelerate the implementation of renewable energy technologies.  In all cases we make specific recommendations for research needs in the near-term (2-5 years), mid-term (5-10 years) and long-term (&gt; 10 years), as well as projecting a timeline for maturation of each technol. soln.  We also identify a no. of priority themes in basic energy science that cut across the entire spectrum.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.2085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Larcher, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span>. <i>Nat. Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">19</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">29</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nchem.2085</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAHBB</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1755-4330</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01388b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium-ion batteries. A look into the future</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">3287</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c1ee01388b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-ion batteries. A look into the future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Scrosati, Bruno; Hassoun, Jusef; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3287-3295</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  A crit. overview of the latest developments in the lithium ion batteries technol. is reported.  We first describe the evolution in the electrolyte area with particular attention to ionic liqs., discussing the expected application of these room temp. molten salts and listing the issues that still prevent their practical implementation.  The attention is then focused on the electrode materials presently considered the most promising for enhancing the energy d. of the batteries.  At the anode side a discussion is provided on the status of development of high capacity tin and silicon lithium alloys.  We show that the morphol. that is the most likely to ensure com. exploitation of these alloy electrodes is that involving carbon-based nanocomposites.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention for large-scale energy storage systems as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the huge availability and low-cost. Herein, we report an efficient hydrothermal method for fabricating hierarchical porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) microspheres assembled from interconnected nanoflakes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, to create efficient charge transfer, we propose a 3D hierarchical meso- and macroporous NVP-based hybrid cathode concept with connected Na ion/electron pathways for ultrafast charge and discharge NIBs, consisting of NVP nanocrystals coated with amorphous carbon and then wrapped by reduced graphene oxide nanosheets (abbreviated as NVP@C@rGO). Here, a cross-linked graphene-caged Na3V2(PO4)2F3 microcubes (NVPF@rGO) composite via a one-pot hydrothermal strategy followed by freeze drying and heat treatment is reported.  As a cathode for a sodium-ion half-cell, the NVPF@rGO delivers excellent cycling stability and rate capability, as well as good low temperature adaptability. The use of fast magic angle spinning (MAS) speeds of 65 kHz allows for the collection of high-resoln. 23Na NMR spectra that reveal two unique peaks at +450 and -175 ppm, corresponding to the two crystallog. unique Na sites in the material of interest.  Two-dimensional NMR exchange spectroscopy results reveal that chem. exchange between the Na ions residing in distinct environments has a max. hopping rate of ∼200 Hz.  The collection of 1-dimensional NMR spectra as a function of electrochem. cycling reveals the reproducible formation of a new peak at +320 ppm in the 23Na NMR spectrum at all intermediate states of charge.  The appearance of this resonance at +320 ppm is attributed to the fully oxidized (NaFePO4F) phase that is present even upon initial electrochem. oxidn.  The simultaneous existence of both the pristine and oxidized phases suggest formation of two distinct phases upon charging, consistent with a two-phase desodiation mechanism.  This two-phase arrangement of Na ions persists for multiple charge/discharge cycles and is congruent with high reversibility of Na (de)intercalation in Na2FePO4F cathodes.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Polyanionic Na3V2(PO4)2F3 with a NASICON-type structure is heralded as a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries due to its fast ionic conduction, high working voltage, and favorable structural stability.  However, a number of challenging issues remain regarding its rate capability and cycle life, which must be addressed to enable greater application compatibility.  Here, a facile and effective approach that can be used to overcome these disadvantages by introducing an aqueous carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder is reported.  The resulting conductive network serves to accelerate the diffusion of Na(+) ions across the interface as well as in the bulk.  The strong binding force of the CMC and stable solid permeable interface protect the electrode from degradation, leading to an excellent capacity of 75 mA h g(-1) at an ultrahigh rate of 70 C (1 C = 128 mA g(-1)) and a long lifespan of 3500 cycles at 30 C while sustaining 79% of the initial capacity value.  A full cell based on this electrode material delivers an impressive energy density as high as 216 W h kg(-1), indicating the potential for application of this straightforward and cost-effective route for the future development of advanced battery technologies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00241"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ling, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hencz, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ling, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exploring Chemical, Mechanical, and Electrical Functionalities of Binders for Advanced Energy-Storage Devices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Hao; Ling, Min; Hencz, Luke; Ling, Han Yeu; Li, Gaoran; Lin, Zhan; Liu, Gao; Zhang, Shanqing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tremendous efforts have been devoted to the development of electrode materials, electrolytes, and separators of energy-storage devices to address the fundamental needs of emerging technologies such as elec. vehicles, artificial intelligence, and virtual reality.  However, binders, as an important component of energy-storage devices, are yet to receive similar attention.  Polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) has been the dominant binder in the battery industry for decades despite several well-recognized drawbacks, i.e., limited binding strength due to the lack of chem. bonds with electroactive materials, insufficient mech. properties, and low electronic and lithium-ion conductivities.  The limited binding function cannot meet inherent demands of emerging electrode materials with high capacities such as silicon anodes and sulfur cathodes.  To address these concerns, in this review we divide the binding between active materials and binders into two major mechanisms: mech. interlocking and interfacial binding forces.  We review existing and emerging binders, binding technol. used in energy-storage devices (including lithium-ion batteries, lithium-sulfur batteries, sodium-ion batteries, and supercapacitors), and state-of-the-art mech. characterization and computational methods for binder research.  Finally, we propose prospective next-generation binders for energy-storage devices from the mol. level to the macro level.  Functional binders will play crucial roles in future high-performance energy-storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1149/1.3526598"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, J.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Geiculescu, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">DesMarteau, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Creager, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionomer Binders Can Improve Discharge Rate Capability in Lithium-Ion Battery Cathodes</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A lithium-ion form of a perfluorosulfonate ionomer was used as a binder in LiFePO4-based lithium-ion battery cathodes. Cathodes were tested against Li4Ti5O12 anodes using 1.0 M and 0.1 M LiPF6-ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC) electrolytes. At low discharge rates (e.g., C/5) both cathode types exhibited similar charge-discharge capacities and voltage profiles. This phenomenon was more pronounced when the salt concn. in the electrolyte was low (e.g., 0.1 M LiPF6-EC/DEC).  These findings suggest that use of ionic binders can help to compensate for electrolyte depletion from the electrode porous space as lithium ions are intercalated into lithium-deficient LiFePO4 particles during rapid discharging.         </div><div class="casAbstract">High-capacity SiO powder composite electrodes for rechargeable Li-ion batteries are prepd. with different polymer binders of poly(acrylic acid) (PAA), poly(vinyl alc.) (PVA), Na CM-cellulose (CMCNa), and conventional poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF).  Electrode performance of the SiO composites depends on the binder, and their electrochem. reversibility is improved by using PAA as the binder in comparison to the PVdF, CMCNa, and PVA binders.  Coulombic efficiency at the initial cycle is improved for the SiO-PAA composite electrode, and the reversible capacity reaches 700-750 mA-h/g for continuous fifty cycling test at a rate of 100 mA/g.  The improvement mechanism of SiO-PAA composite electrode is characterized by x-ray diffraction, electron microscopy, XPS, IR spectroscopy, self-discharge test, and adhesive strength test.  Amorphous PAA polymer not only tightly binds but also covers the individual SiO particles.  Also, the PAA binder suppresses swelling of the composite electrode with the electrolyte soln. compared to the PVdF binder.  Through-thickness elec. resistance of the PAA composite electrode is much lower than that of the PVdF when it is wet with the electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The investigation on the cathode material of potassium ion batteries (PIBs), one of the most promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries, is of great significance.  Potassium vanadium fluorophosphate (KVPO4F) with a high working voltage is an appealing cathode candidate for PIBs, while the poor cycling performance and low electronic cond. dramatically hinder the application. In addn., it maintains 83.6% of its initial capacity at 50 mA g-1 after 100 cycles at 55 °C.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Traditional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) forming additives of vinylene carbonate (VC), fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and ethylene sulfite (ES) are studied with respect to their impact on the formation and growth of the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) layer.  T-half cells are assembled and undergo three different electrochem. study plans: after formation (0.1 C, 5 cycles) and long term cycling (0.1 C, 5 const. current cycles + 1C, 100/150 const. current/voltage cycles), SEM, XRD, XPS and gas chromatog.-mass spectrometry (GC-MS) are combined to study morphol., CEI compn., CEI thickness and aging products for cells with different electrolyte systems.  The obtained results reveal a significant influence of these additives on the CEI compn. and CEI growth.  With the help of SEM, large areas of electrolyte decompn. products are formed at the aged electrode surfaces ( = after cycling), with the exception when 2 vol.% of FEC is added into the ref. electrolyte.  From XPS measurements, CEI thicknesses are calcd.  The ref. electrolyte with 2 vol.% of FEC shows the thinnest layer after long time aging (0.8 ± 0.2 nm).  For the addn. of 2 vol.% of VC, an incremental growth of the CEI thickness occurs from the 100th to 150th cycle (from 1.0 ± 0.1 nm to 2.9 ± 0.4 nm).  By correlating the CEI thickness values with the electrochem. performance, for lithium metal based half cells, the existence of a thinner CEI layer corresponds to a better cycling behavior, with 2 vol.% of FEC showing the highest discharge capacity of 114.4 ± 0.2 mAh/g after 150 cycles at 1C.  GC-MS shows that both VC and FEC help to prevent fast electrolyte aging.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.124407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rapidly synthesizing interconnected carbon nanocage by microwave toward high-performance aluminum batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>389</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">124407</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2020.124407</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rapidly synthesizing interconnected carbon nanocage by microwave toward high-performance aluminum batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Erjin; Wang, Bin; Wang, Jue; Ding, Hongbo; Zhang, Shi; Duan, Huigao; Yu, Xinzhi; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">389</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">124407</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cathode materials with specific physicochem. properties are key to the excellent electrochem. performance of aluminum batteries.  Here, we propose a catalyst-free microwave pulse radiation method to simply and rapidly convert Ketjen black into inter-connected carbon nanocage.  The product has a three-dimensional inter-connected network framework, a large sp. surface area, and eximious structural stability, which is beneficial for industrial development applications.  Besides, mol. dynamics simulations show that when inter-connected carbon nanocage is used to AlCl4- anion storage, it has a self-protection mechanism, which can avoid structure collapse caused by excessive anion embedding.  The inter-connected carbon nanocage exhibits excellent discharge capacity of 117 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 1000 mA g-1 and exceptional stability with a discharge capacity as high as 105 mAh g-1 at 2000 mA g-1 after 1000 cycles as the cathode for the aluminum battery.  The study greatly increases the prodn. speed and reduces the requirements of exptl. conditions for carbon nanocage, which may lead to a significant breakthrough for rapid, large-scale prodn. of aluminum battery cathode materials and other fields.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14294?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14294</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of the NVPOF electrode using different binders in the fresh state and after 200 cycles at 1C; pressure test for the NVPOF electrodes; GITT profiles of every titration period for the NVPOF cathode; electrochemical characteristics for the CC anode; electrochemical performance comparison (Table S1); and impedance parameters (Table S2) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14294/suppl_file/am0c14294_si_001.pdf">am0c14294_si_001.pdf (1.25 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

    adroll_adv_id = "3LBZJ4KXKBF2PJ46QCMT3X";
    adroll_pix_id = "6EITYRDI4FFGJPSZQ62UJJ";

        var _onload = function(){

<script nonce="9318805cccd854b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318805cccd854b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {

          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");

      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="9318805cccd854b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });

                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";

    });
});
